Refuting the Fourth Objection: Expulsion from the Garden
The fourth Mu'tazilite objection is presented, but the author's answer is missing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth Mu'tazilite objection regarding the expulsion from the Garden coinciding with Adam's slip, even though the author's response is missing from the text.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that the expulsion coinciding with the slip makes it appear as punishment, but the answer is not preserved.

The fourth objection states that the expulsion from the Garden should not be attributed to Adam's slip because the expulsion happened to coincide with it, making it seem like punishment. Unfortunately, the author's answer to this objection is not extant in the source text. We will examine the objection itself and consider what a response might entail.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the fourth objection. Note that there is no author response following it in the text.
The text only contains the objection, then moves to the fifth.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the expulsion of people from the Garden (due to Adam's sin) should not be said to be a punishment, because the expulsion coincided with the slip only apparently, not causally.
The wording is fragmented: 'ما كان يجوز أن يقال' (it should not be said).
3
Consider the missing answer
Since the author's answer is absent, we can only speculate. Typical responses might argue that the expulsion was indeed a consequence or that God's actions are not bound by human categories.
This lesson highlights the importance of textual completeness.

Refuting the Fifth Objection: Argument for Pharaoh and Haman
Author refutes the comparison to Pharaoh and Haman by distinguishing human debate from God's sovereign decision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that if Adam's excuse (predestination) were valid, it would also excuse Pharaoh and Haman.

Core Idea
God accepts excuses from some but not others based on His absolute will, not on a general principle.

The fifth objection is powerful: if Adam could cite predestination as an excuse, why can't Pharaoh and Haman do the same? The author responds by limiting the debate to humans and asserting that God, as the absolute sovereign, may accept an excuse from Adam and reject it from others. This lesson explores that nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the full Arabic and translation. Notice the length of the answer compared to earlier ones.
This answer is more detailed.
2
Identify the condition
The author says the argument (that predestination is an excuse) is valid only when the debate is between humans. Since the debate is between God and humans, God can decide.
3
Understand the divine prerogative
God says: I do not accept this excuse from Pharaoh and Haman, but I accept it from Adam. The reason: 'my actions are based solely on ulūhiyya' (divine lordship).
This grounds God's discretion in His nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب :
أما قوله : « رواية أبي هريرة مضطربة في هذا الخبر قلنا : جميع الروايات مشتملة على التعليل بتقدير الله . ودليلنا : هو هذا القدر المشترك . قوله ثانياً : كيف يشافه الولد والده بالسوء ، لا سيما إذا كان الولد من
أكابر الأنبياء ؟ ، قلنا : لا نسلم أن ذلك من باب المشافهة بالسوء . بل كان ذلك من باب الشكاية .
قوله ثالثاً : : الصادر من آدم كان من باب الصغائر ، فلا يجوز أن يلام عليه ، قلنا : لا نسلم أنه كان من باب الصغائر ، بل كان من باب الكبائر . إلا أنا نقول : إن تلك الواقعة وقعت قبل النبوة
بسبب آدم ، قلنا : لم لا
وقوله رابعاً : « خروج الناس من الجنة ، ما كان يجوز أن يقال : لما حصل الإخراج مقارناً لتلك الزلمة ، انضاف إليها بحسب
الظاهر .
وقوله خامساً : ( لو كان ذلك حجة لآدم ، لكان أيضاً حجة لفرعون وهامان ، قلنا : هذه المناظرة إنما وقعت بين آدم وبين موسى . وهذه الحجة قوية بشرط أن تقع المناظرة بين إنسانين . إلا أن عندنا المناظرة مع الله ممتنعة . فلله - سبحانه - أن يقول : هذا العذر ، وإن كان عذراً فيها بين الخلق . إلا أني لا أقبله من فرعون وهامان ، وأقبله من آدم . لأن أفعالي معللة بمحض الإلهية وقوله ( سادساً (۱) ] : ( لعله - عليه السلام ـ إنما حكى هذا الكلام عن
اليهود ، قلنا : هذا سوء ظن بالراوي وقوله سابعاً : « لعل الإعراب بخلاف ما ذكرتم ، قلنا : هذا أيضاً سوء
ظن بالراوي .
وقوله ثامناً : « لعل المراد بهذا التقدير : أنه تعالى كتب ذلك على آدم ، قلنا : هب أنه كذلك. لكن الله تعالى لما أخبر عنه ، امتنع أن لا يقع ، وإلا لزم أن ينقلب خبر الله من كونه صدقاً إلى كونه كذباً ، وإنه محال
واعلم : أنه لما رجع حاصل الاستدلال بهذا الخبر إلى هذا الحرف . فتقول : هذا الحرف حاصل ، بدون التمسك بهذا الخبر . لأن القرآن دل على أنه تعالى أخبر عن بعضهم ، أنه لا يؤمن . حينئذ يتم هذا الاستدلال ، من غير
And the answer:
As for his saying: 'The narration of Abu Hurayra is inconsistent in this report,' we said: All the narrations include the explanation by God's decree. And our proof is this common element.
His second saying: 'How can a son speak abusively to his father, especially when the son is among the greatest prophets?' We said: We do not accept that this is a case of abusive speech. Rather, it was a complaint.
His third saying: 'What issued from Adam was of the minor sins, so it is not permissible to blame him for it.' We said: We do not accept that it was of the minor sins; rather, it was of the major sins. Except that we say: That incident occurred before prophethood. 'Because of Adam,' we said: Why not?
And his fourth saying: 'The expulsion of people from the Garden should not be said... because the expulsion coinciding with that slip was added to it apparently.'
And his fifth saying: 'If that were an argument for Adam, it would also be an argument for Pharaoh and Haman.' We said: This debate only occurred between Adam and Moses. And this argument is strong on the condition that the debate occurs between two humans. However, in our view, debate with God is impossible. For God—glorified—can say: This excuse, even if it is an excuse among creatures, I do not accept it from Pharaoh and Haman, but I accept it from Adam. Because my actions are based solely on ulūhiyya (divine lordship).
And his sixth saying: 'Perhaps he—peace be upon him—merely narrated this speech from the Jews.' We said: This is a bad opinion of the narrator.
And his seventh saying: 'Perhaps the grammatical inflection is contrary to what you mentioned.' We said: This is also a bad opinion of the narrator.
And his eighth saying: 'Perhaps what is meant by this taqdīr is that God wrote that upon Adam.' We said: Granted that. But since God informed about it, it is impossible for it not to occur; otherwise, it would necessitate that God's news turns from truth to falsehood, which is impossible.
And know: when the essence of the inference by this report reduces to this point, you say: This point is established without adhering to this report. For the Qur'an indicates that God informed about some of them that they will not believe. Then this inference is complete, without...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is the author's systematic refutation of each Mu'tazilite objection, followed by a concluding independent argument.

The author answers eight points:
1. To the claim that Abu Hurayra's narration is inconsistent, he responds that all versions share the common element of attributing the matter to God's decree.
2. To the objection that a son should not speak abusively to his father, he says it was not abuse but a complaint (shikāya).
3. To the claim that Adam's sin was minor and thus not blameworthy, the author asserts it was a major sin, but argues it occurred before prophethood (thus before his infallibility). The response ends abruptly.
4. The author's brief response to the objection about expulsion coinciding with the slip is cut off; the text only states the objection partially.
5. To the objection that if Adam's excuse were valid it would justify Pharaoh and Haman, the author distinguishes: this debate was between humans, and God may accept excuses from some and not others by His absolute will.
6. To the suggestion that the Prophet merely relayed a Jewish story, he labels it a bad opinion of the narrator.
7. To the grammatical argument, he again calls it a bad opinion of the narrator.
8. To the claim that taqdīr means writing in the Preserved Tablet, he argues that even so, once God informs of something, its occurrence is necessary; otherwise God's word would become false, which is impossible.

Finally, the author concludes that the same conclusion (that the disbelievers are not excused due to predestination) can be derived directly from the Qur'an (e.g., verses stating God informed that some will not believe), without relying on the hadith. The text cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
المناظرة
الحجة
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the argument from predestination not excuse Pharaoh and Haman?
A) Because they were greater sinners than Adam.
B) Because the debate is between humans, and God may choose to accept or reject excuses based on His divine will.
C) Because predestination does not exist in their case.
D) Because they did not repent.

Key Takeaway
God's acceptance of predestination as an excuse is not universal; He exercises His sovereign discretion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone who persists in sin but still receives God's mercy, does this lesson challenge your sense of fairness? Are you willing to accept that God's ways are not bound by your logic? What judgment in your own heart do you need to repent of today?
?
Have you ever used the excuse of predestination to justify a sin or to avoid taking responsibility? How does this lesson force you to reconsider? Will you now commit to repent without conditions, trusting God's discretion over your own reasoning?
?
God says, 'My actions are based solely on ulūhiyya.' Does this truth unsettle you or bring you peace? What must change in your relationship with God so that you submit completely to His absolute sovereignty, even when you don't understand His decisions?

Refuting the Sixth and Seventh Objections: Bad Opinion of the Narrator
Author answers two objections concerning the narrator's reliability and grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's dismissal of two objections that cast doubt on the narrator or the grammar of the hadith, labeling them as baseless suspicion.

Core Idea
Accusing the narrator of poor transmission is unwarranted; the hadith is sound.

The sixth and seventh objections attempt to undermine the hadith by suggesting that the Prophet (peace be on him) merely repeated a Jewish story, or that the grammatical inflection (i‘rāb) is different, changing the meaning. The author dismisses both as 'bad opinion of the narrator' (سوء ظن بالراوي), showing his confidence in the transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections and answers
Read the short exchanges. Notice the author's consistent response: 'this is a bad opinion of the narrator.'
Both answers are identical in structure.
2
Understand the sixth objection
The Mu'tazila claimed the Prophet might have been quoting Jewish sources. The author rejects this as a baseless assumption.
This would imply the hadith is not authentic.
3
Understand the seventh objection
The Mu'tazila suggested a different grammatical reading to alter the meaning. Again, the author dismisses it as unfair to the narrator.
The author holds the apparent meaning.

Refuting the Eighth Objection: Taqdir as Writing in the Tablet
Author refutes the claim that taqdīr refers only to writing, not predestination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the claim that 'taqdīr' merely means God wrote it in the Preserved Tablet, and the logical consequence that God's news must occur.

Core Idea
Even if taqdīr is writing, God's news about it is absolute; otherwise His truth would become false.

The eighth objection tries to reinterpret 'taqdīr' as simply God's writing in the Preserved Tablet, implying no binding predestination. The author concedes the possibility, but then argues that the key issue is that God informed about this writing; if what He informed about does not happen, His news would become false—an impossibility. This shifts the debate from predestination to God's veracity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the author's concession: 'hubbu annahu kadhaalik' (granted that).
The answer uses a 'hubbu' technique: conceding the point for argument.
2
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazila say: 'taqdīr' means God wrote it in the Tablet, not that He decrees it. This would reduce the force of the hadith.
3
Analyze the author's logical move
The author says: even if that is the case, God informed us that He wrote that. If what He wrote does not happen, then God's news would turn from truth to falsehood, which is impossible. Therefore, the occurrence of what was written is necessary.
This argument does not depend on the meaning of taqdīr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if taqdīr only means 'writing', then predestination is not binding and events can change.
Correction: Even if taqdīr is writing, God's news that He wrote it makes its occurrence necessary; otherwise God would be false.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would happen if what God wrote in the Tablet does not occur?
A) It would be a miracle.
B) God's news would turn from truth to falsehood, which is impossible.
C) It would prove the hadith is weak.
D) It would show that God changed His mind.

Key Takeaway
Even accepting the Mu'tazilite reinterpretation of taqdīr, God's veracity guarantees that what He informed about must occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God's decrees as if they are merely 'written' stories in a book, feeling distant from their power? How does the author's argument—that God's news about your life is absolutely true—challenge that detachment? Will you today start reading the Qur'an as if every promise and warning is a direct news report that must come to pass?
?
The objectors twisted taqdir to avoid God's absolute sovereignty over their actions. In what subtle ways do you twist your understanding of qadar to avoid taking responsibility for your choices? What would it cost you to truly believe that God's news about your deeds is unchangeable and that you are still fully accountable?

Concluding Independent Argument from the Qur'an
Author shows the same conclusion can be reached via Qur'an, making the hadith unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's concluding point that the same conclusion—that disbelievers are not excused due to predestination—can be derived directly from the Qur'an without relying on the hadith.

Core Idea
The Qur'an itself states that God informed about some disbelievers, so the argument stands independently.

After refuting all eight objections, the author adds a powerful closing note: even if the hadith were not used, the same point can be proven from the Qur'an. He references verses where God informs that some people will not believe. This shows the argument is not dependent on a single hadith, and it also addresses the Mu'tazilite challenge to the hadith's authenticity.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the phrase 'this point is established without adhering to this report.'
The text cuts off at the end, but the main idea is clear.
2
Understand the logic
The author says the essence of the hadith's inference reduces to a simple point: God informed about some people that they will not believe. The Qur'an contains such information (e.g., 'God has sealed their hearts').
3
Recognize the methodological value
This shows that the author's position on predestination and excuse is not based solely on a potentially contested hadith, but on clear Qur'anic verses.
This strengthens the overall argument.

ظن بالراوي .
وقوله ثامناً : « لعل المراد بهذا التقدير : أنه تعالى كتب ذلك على آدم ، قلنا : هب أنه كذلك. لكن الله تعالى لما أخبر عنه ، امتنع أن لا يقع ، وإلا لزم أن ينقلب خبر الله من كونه صدقاً إلى كونه كذباً ، وإنه محال
واعلم : أنه لما رجع حاصل الاستدلال بهذا الخبر إلى هذا الحرف . فتقول : هذا الحرف حاصل ، بدون التمسك بهذا الخبر . لأن القرآن دل على أنه تعالى أخبر عن بعضهم ، أنه لا يؤمن . حينئذ يتم هذا الاستدلال ، من غير
And the answer:
As for his saying: 'The narration of Abu Hurayra is inconsistent in this report,' we said: All the narrations include the explanation by God's decree. And our proof is this common element.
His second saying: 'How can a son speak abusively to his father, especially when the son is among the greatest prophets?' We said: We do not accept that this is a case of abusive speech. Rather, it was a complaint.
His third saying: 'What issued from Adam was of the minor sins, so it is not permissible to blame him for it.' We said: We do not accept that it was of the minor sins; rather, it was of the major sins. Except that we say: That incident occurred before prophethood. 'Because of Adam,' we said: Why not?
And his fourth saying: 'The expulsion of people from the Garden should not be said... because the expulsion coinciding with that slip was added to it apparently.'
And his fifth saying: 'If that were an argument for Adam, it would also be an argument for Pharaoh and Haman.' We said: This debate only occurred between Adam and Moses. And this argument is strong on the condition that the debate occurs between two humans. However, in our view, debate with God is impossible. For God—glorified—can say: This excuse, even if it is an excuse among creatures, I do not accept it from Pharaoh and Haman, but I accept it from Adam. Because my actions are based solely on ulūhiyya (divine lordship).
And his sixth saying: 'Perhaps he—peace be upon him—merely narrated this speech from the Jews.' We said: This is a bad opinion of the narrator.
And his seventh saying: 'Perhaps the grammatical inflection is contrary to what you mentioned.' We said: This is also a bad opinion of the narrator.
And his eighth saying: 'Perhaps what is meant by this taqdīr is that God wrote that upon Adam.' We said: Granted that. But since God informed about it, it is impossible for it not to occur; otherwise, it would necessitate that God's news turns from truth to falsehood, which is impossible.
And know: when the essence of the inference by this report reduces to this point, you say: This point is established without adhering to this report. For the Qur'an indicates that God informed about some of them that they will not believe. Then this inference is complete, without...
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is the author's systematic refutation of each Mu'tazilite objection, followed by a concluding independent argument.

The author answers eight points:
1. To the claim that Abu Hurayra's narration is inconsistent, he responds that all versions share the common element of attributing the matter to God's decree.
2. To the objection that a son should not speak abusively to his father, he says it was not abuse but a complaint (shikāya).
3. To the claim that Adam's sin was minor and thus not blameworthy, the author asserts it was a major sin, but argues it occurred before prophethood (thus before his infallibility). The response ends abruptly.
4. The author's brief response to the objection about expulsion coinciding with the slip is cut off; the text only states the objection partially.
5. To the objection that if Adam's excuse were valid it would justify Pharaoh and Haman, the author distinguishes: this debate was between humans, and God may accept excuses from some and not others by His absolute will.
6. To the suggestion that the Prophet merely relayed a Jewish story, he labels it a bad opinion of the narrator.
7. To the grammatical argument, he again calls it a bad opinion of the narrator.
8. To the claim that taqdīr means writing in the Preserved Tablet, he argues that even so, once God informs of something, its occurrence is necessary; otherwise God's word would become false, which is impossible.

Finally, the author concludes that the same conclusion (that the disbelievers are not excused due to predestination) can be derived directly from the Qur'an (e.g., verses stating God informed that some will not believe), without relying on the hadith. The text cuts off mid-sentence.

القرآن
الحرف

Correction: The author shows the same point can be made from the Qur'an, so the hadith is not essential.

Checkpoint

B) That Allah informed about some people that they will not believe.
C) That Adam was the first prophet.

Key Takeaway
The core argument that predestination does not excuse disbelief is independently supported by the Qur'an.

?

Refutation of the Claim That Adam's Sin Was Minor
Objection and refutation regarding the severity of Adam's sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that Adam's sin was minor by asserting it was major but occurred before prophethood.

Core Idea
Adam's sin was major, but its timing before prophethood alters its blameworthiness.

The third objection against the Adam-Moses argument claims that Adam's sin was a minor sin and thus not blameworthy. The author counters by denying that it was minor, stating it was a major sin, but adds that it occurred before Adam's prophethood, which changes the context. This lesson examines the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation of the third objection and its refutation. Notice the structure: first the objector's claim, then the author's response.
2
Identify the key claims
The objector says the sin was minor (ṣaghīra), so no blame. The author says it was major (kabīra) but occurred before prophethood.
Prophethood is significant because prophets are protected from major sins after it.
3
Reflect on the significance of timing
Why does the author mention that the incident was before prophethood? Think about how this changes the level of accountability.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ثالثاً : الصادر من آدم كان من باب الصغائر ، فلا يجوز أن يلام عليه ، قلنا : لا نسلم أنه كان من باب الصغائر ، بل كان من باب الكبائر . إلا أنا نقول : إن تلك الواقعة وقعت قبل النبوة . وقوله رابعاً : خروج الناس من الجنة ، ما كان يجوز أن يقال : لما حصل الإخراج مقارناً لتلك الزلمة ، انضاف إليها بحسب الظاهر . وقوله خامساً : لو كان ذلك حجة لآدم ، لكان أيضاً حجة لفرعون وهامان ، قلنا : هذه المناظرة إنما وقعت بين آدم وبين موسى . وهذه الحجة قوية بشرط أن تقع المناظرة بين إنسانين . إلا أن عندنا المناظرة مع الله ممتنعة . فلله سبحانه أن يقول : هذا العذر ، وإن كان عذراً فيما بين الخلق . إلا أني لا أقبله من فرعون وهامان ، وأقبله من آدم . لأن أفعالي معللة بمحض الإلهية . وقوله سادساً : لعله عليه السلام إنما حكى هذا الكلام عن اليهود ، قلنا : هذا سوء ظن بالراوي . وقوله سابعاً : لعل الإعراب بخلاف ما ذكرتم ، قلنا : هذا أيضاً سوء ظن بالراوي . وقوله ثامناً : لعل المراد بهذا التقدير : أنه تعالى كتب ذلك على آدم ، قلنا : هب أنه كذلك . لكن الله تعالى لما أخبر عنه ، امتنع أن لا يقع ، وإلا لزم أن ينقلب خبر الله من كونه صدقاً إلى كونه كذباً ، وإنه محال .
His third statement: 'What issued from Adam was of the minor sins, so it is not permissible to blame him for it.' We said: 'We do not concede that it was of the minor sins; rather, it was of the major sins. But we say that that incident occurred before prophethood.' And his fourth statement: 'The expulsion of people from Paradise could not be said because the expulsion occurs concomitant with that slip, so it is attributed to it apparently.' And his fifth statement: 'If that were an argument for Adam, it would also be an argument for Pharaoh and Haman.' We said: 'This debate only occurred between Adam and Moses. And this argument is strong on condition that the debate occurs between two humans. But according to us, debate with God is impossible. For God—exalted is He—may say: This excuse, even if it is an excuse among the creation, yet I do not accept it from Pharaoh and Haman, and I accept it from Adam, because my actions are caused purely by divinity.' And his sixth statement: 'Perhaps he—peace be upon him—only related this speech from the Jews.' We said: 'This is a bad assumption about the narrator.' And his seventh statement: 'Perhaps the grammatical parsing is contrary to what you mentioned.' We said: 'This too is a bad assumption about the narrator.' And his eighth statement: 'Perhaps what is meant by this predetermination is that God wrote that upon Adam.' We said: 'Granted that it is so. But when God informed of it, it is impossible that it not occur, otherwise it would entail that God's news turns from being truthful to being false, and that is impossible.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents refutations of six objections against the argument from the debate between Adam and Moses regarding predestination. The author begins by responding to the third objection that Adam's sin was minor and thus not blameworthy. The refutation denies that it was minor, stating it was major, but adds that it occurred before prophethood, implying a different status. The fourth objection claims the expulsion from Paradise cannot be attributed to Adam's sin. The answer likely clarifies the apparent connection. The fifth objection draws an analogy with Pharaoh and Haman. The answer distinguishes between human-to-human debate and debate with God, asserting that God's actions are based on pure divinity and He accepts excuses only from whom He wills. The sixth and seventh objections suggest the narrator or grammar may be faulty; the answer dismisses these as bad assumptions about the narrator. The eighth objection posits that the predetermination means God wrote it upon Adam. The answer affirms that if God informed of it, it must occur, otherwise God's news would become false, which is impossible. Thus, all objections are refuted by showing their internal inconsistency or improper assumptions.

الصغائر
الكبائر
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins of prophets are minor and hence not blameworthy.
Correction: The author shows that Adam's sin was major, but its timing before prophethood is key, and prophets are protected from major sins after prophethood.

Checkpoint

A) He agrees it was minor.
B) He says it was major and occurred before prophethood.
C) He says it was not a sin at all.
D) He says it was a sin but forgiven.

Key Takeaway
The refutation clarifies that Adam's sin was major, not minor, but its occurrence before prophethood is relevant to its assessment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Adam's sin was major, yet he was forgiven and raised to prophethood. Does this give you false comfort that you can sin without urgency, or does it drive you to seek forgiveness immediately, knowing that major sins require decisive repentance?
?
If you were in Adam's position before prophethood, would you have made the same excuse—'I didn't know better'—to avoid blame? What does this lesson teach you about taking full ownership of your sins regardless of your circumstances?

Refutation of the Analogy with Pharaoh and Haman
Refutation of the objection comparing Adam's argument to that of Pharaoh and Haman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between Adam and Pharaoh/Haman fails according to the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
God's acceptance of excuses is not bound by human analogies; His actions are purely divine.

The fifth objection argues that if Adam's excuse were valid, it would also apply to Pharaoh and Haman. The author refutes by distinguishing between human debate and divine decree, asserting that God may accept or reject excuses based on His absolute will. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation of the fifth objection and its refutation. Note the structure: the objection draws an analogy, the answer distinguishes between human and divine contexts.
2
Identify the key point
The author says the argument is strong between humans, but with God, debate is impossible. God's actions are caused by pure divinity (maḥḍ al-ilāhiyya).
This means God is not subject to human logic in accepting excuses.
3
Understand 'محض الإلهية'
Reflect on the phrase 'because my actions are caused purely by divinity.' What does it imply about God's discretion?
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this refutation protects divine justice from being constrained by human analogies.

Refutation of the Claim of Jewish Narration
Refutation of the claim that the hadith is taken from Jewish sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's dismissal of the suggestion that the Adam-Moses story was borrowed from Jewish sources as a baseless assumption.

Core Idea
Doubting the narrator's integrity without evidence is unjustified.

The sixth objection suggests that Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) might have related the story from the Jews rather than divine revelation. The author dismisses this as a bad assumption about the narrator, affirming the trustworthiness of the prophetic narration. This lesson examines that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how short the refutation is: just one phrase.
2
Identify the assumption being criticized
The objector assumes the Prophet might have narrated from Jews. The author calls this a 'bad assumption' (sūʾ ẓann).
This implies it is an unjustified suspicion.
3
Reflect on the principle
Think about the principle of ḥusn al-ẓann (good assumption) regarding narrators of hadith.
4
Why is this objection weak?
Consider that there is no evidence for the claim; it is pure speculation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ثالثاً : الصادر من آدم كان من باب الصغائر ، فلا يجوز أن يلام عليه ، قلنا : لا نسلم أنه كان من باب الصغائر ، بل كان من باب الكبائر . إلا أنا نقول : إن تلك الواقعة وقعت قبل النبوة . وقوله رابعاً : خروج الناس من الجنة ، ما كان يجوز أن يقال : لما حصل الإخراج مقارناً لتلك الزلمة ، انضاف إليها بحسب الظاهر . وقوله خامساً : لو كان ذلك حجة لآدم ، لكان أيضاً حجة لفرعون وهامان ، قلنا : هذه المناظرة إنما وقعت بين آدم وبين موسى . وهذه الحجة قوية بشرط أن تقع المناظرة بين إنسانين . إلا أن عندنا المناظرة مع الله ممتنعة . فلله سبحانه أن يقول : هذا العذر ، وإن كان عذراً فيما بين الخلق . إلا أني لا أقبله من فرعون وهامان ، وأقبله من آدم . لأن أفعالي معللة بمحض الإلهية . وقوله سادساً : لعله عليه السلام إنما حكى هذا الكلام عن اليهود ، قلنا : هذا سوء ظن بالراوي . وقوله سابعاً : لعل الإعراب بخلاف ما ذكرتم ، قلنا : هذا أيضاً سوء ظن بالراوي . وقوله ثامناً : لعل المراد بهذا التقدير : أنه تعالى كتب ذلك على آدم ، قلنا : هب أنه كذلك . لكن الله تعالى لما أخبر عنه ، امتنع أن لا يقع ، وإلا لزم أن ينقلب خبر الله من كونه صدقاً إلى كونه كذباً ، وإنه محال .
His third statement: 'What issued from Adam was of the minor sins, so it is not permissible to blame him for it.' We said: 'We do not concede that it was of the minor sins; rather, it was of the major sins. But we say that that incident occurred before prophethood.' And his fourth statement: 'The expulsion of people from Paradise could not be said because the expulsion occurs concomitant with that slip, so it is attributed to it apparently.' And his fifth statement: 'If that were an argument for Adam, it would also be an argument for Pharaoh and Haman.' We said: 'This debate only occurred between Adam and Moses. And this argument is strong on condition that the debate occurs between two humans. But according to us, debate with God is impossible. For God—exalted is He—may say: This excuse, even if it is an excuse among the creation, yet I do not accept it from Pharaoh and Haman, and I accept it from Adam, because my actions are caused purely by divinity.' And his sixth statement: 'Perhaps he—peace be upon him—only related this speech from the Jews.' We said: 'This is a bad assumption about the narrator.' And his seventh statement: 'Perhaps the grammatical parsing is contrary to what you mentioned.' We said: 'This too is a bad assumption about the narrator.' And his eighth statement: 'Perhaps what is meant by this predetermination is that God wrote that upon Adam.' We said: 'Granted that it is so. But when God informed of it, it is impossible that it not occur, otherwise it would entail that God's news turns from being truthful to being false, and that is impossible.'
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents refutations of six objections against the argument from the debate between Adam and Moses regarding predestination. The author begins by responding to the third objection that Adam's sin was minor and thus not blameworthy. The refutation denies that it was minor, stating it was major, but adds that it occurred before prophethood, implying a different status. The fourth objection claims the expulsion from Paradise cannot be attributed to Adam's sin. The answer likely clarifies the apparent connection. The fifth objection draws an analogy with Pharaoh and Haman. The answer distinguishes between human-to-human debate and debate with God, asserting that God's actions are based on pure divinity and He accepts excuses only from whom He wills. The sixth and seventh objections suggest the narrator or grammar may be faulty; the answer dismisses these as bad assumptions about the narrator. The eighth objection posits that the predetermination means God wrote it upon Adam. The answer affirms that if God informed of it, it must occur, otherwise God's news would become false, which is impossible. Thus, all objections are refuted by showing their internal inconsistency or improper assumptions.

Key Terms
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Adam-Moses hadith may have originated from Jewish traditions.
Correction: The author asserts that this is an unwarranted negative assumption about the Prophet's truthfulness.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that the Prophet related the story from the Jews?
A) He agrees it is possible.
B) He says it is a bad assumption about the narrator.
C) He provides evidence that it is not from Jews.
D) He ignores the objection.

Key Takeaway
Unsubstantiated doubts about the Prophet's narrations are rejected as slander.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself doubting the origin of a hadith because it resembled something from another tradition? How does this passage's blunt refutation—'this is a bad assumption about the narrator'—expose the arrogance in your heart? Will you repent and affirm that the Prophet's speech is pure revelation?

Refutation of the Grammatical Parsing Objection
Refutation of the objection based on alternative grammatical analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author rejects grammatical reinterpretations that undermine the predestination argument.

Core Idea
Changing the grammar to avoid the argument is a bad assumption about the narrator.

The seventh objection proposes that a different grammatical parsing of the hadith could alter its meaning and weaken the predestination argument. The author dismisses this as another bad assumption about the narrator, insisting that the apparent meaning is what matters. This lesson explores that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this objection is similar in form to the sixth.
2
Understand 'grammatical parsing'
The objector suggests that the iʿrāb (case endings) could be different, changing the meaning. Why might that undermine the argument?
3
Note the author's principle
The author again says it is a bad assumption about the narrator. This implies that the apparent reading should be trusted.
4
Reflect on textual integrity
Consider the importance of not changing the text based on speculation.

Refutation of the Interpretation of Predetermination as Mere Writing
Refutation of the interpretation that predetermination is just writing without necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that if God decreed something, it must necessarily occur, to avoid implying falsity in God's news.

Core Idea
If God informs of something, its occurrence is inevitable to preserve His truthfulness.

The eighth objection interprets predetermination as merely God writing something upon Adam, not that it must necessarily occur. The author counters that if God has informed of it, then it must happen; otherwise, God's news would turn false, which is impossible. This lesson examines that logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the author grants the interpretation for argument's sake ('Granted that it is so').
2
Identify the core logical principle
The author uses the principle: God's news must be truthful. If He informs of something, it cannot fail to occur.
3
Understand the impossibility of falsity in God
Why is it impossible for God's news to become false? Because God is all-knowing and truthful.
4
Reflect on the implications for predestination
Consider how this argument strengthens the case for predestination: what God decrees and informs about must happen.

Hadith on Four Beliefs and Quranic Order
Hadith on four beliefs and its agreement with Quranic order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hadith on four essential beliefs aligns with the thematic order in Surat al-Baqarah.

Core Idea
The hadith's four beliefs match the Quran's own order, confirming authenticity.

This lesson presents a second proof: a hadith from Abu Dawud that lists four beliefs essential for true faith. Imam al-Razi then demonstrates that the order of these beliefs—tawhid, prophethood, resurrection, and predestination—corresponds exactly to the sequence of topics at the beginning of Surat al-Baqarah. This alignment strengthens the hadith's authority and shows the coherence of Islamic teachings.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the hadith from Ali ibn Abi Talib, noting the four beliefs listed. Understand that without these, faith is incomplete.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud's Sunan.
2
Examine Imam al-Razi's argument
See how he pairs each belief with a Quranic verse from Surat al-Baqarah in the same order: tawhid (2:21), prophethood (2:23), resurrection (2:25), and predestination (2:26).
The verses are in the same sequence as the hadith.
3
Reflect on the significance
The correspondence between hadith and Quranic order indicates that the hadith is consistent with divine revelation, reinforcing its reliability as a source of doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : روى أبو داود في سننه ، بإسناده ، عن علي بن أبي طالب قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا يؤمن عبد ، حتى يؤمن بأربع : يشهد أن لا إله إلا الله . وأني رسول الله بعثني بالحق. ويؤمن بالبعث بعد الموت . ويؤمن بالقدر ، وزاد بعضهم : ( خيره وشره ) . قال الإمام الداعي إلى الله ، فخر الملة والدين ، محمد الرازي ، ختم له بالحسنى : هذا الترتيب موافق لكتاب الله . وذلك لأنه تعالى فصل أحوال الفرق الثلاثة في أول سورة البقرة . وهم المؤمنون والكافرون والمنافقون . ثم ابتدأ بدلائل التوحيد ، فقال : ( اعبدوا ربكم الذي خلقكم ) ثم ثني بدلائل النبوة فقال : ( وإن كنتم في ريب مما نزلنا ) ثم ثلث بأمر البعث والقيامة ، فقال : ( وبشر الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات : أن لهم جنات ) ثم ربع بأمر القدر ، فقال : ( يضل به كثيراً ، ويهدي به كثيراً ) فصارت الأمور الأربعة المذكورة في الخبر ، هي الأمور المذكورة في أول كتاب الله تعالى بذلك الترتيب من غير فرق .
The second proof: Abu Dawud narrated in his Sunan with his chain from Ali ibn Abi Talib who said: The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'A servant does not believe until he believes in four: that he bears witness that there is no god but God, and that I am the Messenger of God sent with the truth; and he believes in resurrection after death; and he believes in predestination.' Some added: 'its good and its evil.' Imam al-Da'i ila Allah, Fakhr al-Milla wa al-Din, Muhammad al-Razi—may God seal his life with good—said: 'This order is in agreement with the Book of God. That is because God—exalted is He—detailed the conditions of the three groups at the beginning of Surat al-Baqarah: the believers, the disbelievers, and the hypocrites. Then He began with proofs of tawhid saying: "Worship your Lord who created you" (2:21). Then He followed with proofs of prophethood saying: "And if you are in doubt about what We have sent down" (2:23). Then He thirdly addressed the matter of resurrection and the Hour saying: "And give good tidings to those who believe and do righteous deeds that they will have gardens" (2:25). Then He fourthly addressed the matter of predestination saying: "He leads astray many thereby and guides many thereby" (2:26). So the four matters mentioned in the hadith are the matters mentioned at the beginning of God's Book in that order without difference.'
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof: a hadith from Abu Dawud listing four essential beliefs that a servant must hold to have true faith. Imam al-Razi then demonstrates that the order of these beliefs—tawhid, prophethood, resurrection, and predestination—matches the order of topics in the opening of Surat al-Baqarah. The Quran begins by describing three groups (believers, disbelievers, hypocrites), then presents proofs for tawhid, then prophethood, then resurrection, and finally predestination. This alignment confirms that the hadith is consistent with the Quran and thus strengthens its authenticity and importance.

الإيمان
الأربع
التوحيد
النبوة
البعث
القدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think predestination is less important because it's listed last.
Correction: The order in both hadith and Quran shows predestination is an integral part of faith, not an afterthought.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth matter mentioned in the hadith according to the order given?
A) Belief in tawhid
B) Belief in prophethood
C) Belief in resurrection
D) Belief in predestination

Key Takeaway
The order of the four essential beliefs in this hadith is exactly the same as the order of topics in the opening of Surat al-Baqarah, confirming its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The hadith says a servant does not truly believe until he believes in all four. Look at the first one: 'I bear witness that there is no god but God.' When you say this, do you feel the weight of accountability that flows from the other three? Or have you made it a comfortable slogan?

The Linguistic Range of Qadar: Beyond Creation
Refutation that qadar means only creation; evidence for meanings of writing and judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic word 'qadar' encompasses meanings of writing and judgment in addition to creation, and how this refutes a narrow interpretation.

Core Idea
Qadar means more than creation—it also means writing and judgment.

This passage tackles a common objection: does the term 'qadar' in the hadith refer only to creation? The author argues no, and provides linguistic evidence from poetry and the Quran. By examining these sources, we see that 'qadar' carries a broader semantic range, including divine decree and judgment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The author rejects the claim that 'qadar' means only creation. Read the opening sentence carefully.
This sets the stage for the linguistic evidence that follows.
2
Examine the poetic evidence
A poet's line is cited where 'qadar' refers to writing in scriptures. Notice how the context demands the meaning of decree.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Two verses about Lot's wife use 'qaddarna' to mean judgment. Compare the translation provided.
The author explicitly states 'meaning We judged.'
4
Synthesize the three meanings
List the three possible meanings: creation, writing, judgment. Understand why the narrow view fails.
This connects to the learning objective.

إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لا نسلم أن المراد بالقدر : الخلق ، بل لفظ القدر ، قد يستعمل أيضاً في الكتابة . قال الشاعر : واعلم : بأن ذا الجلال قد قدر في الصحف الأولى ، التي كان سطر . ويستعمل أيضاً في الحكم. قال تعالى : ( إلا امرأته قدرناها من الغابرين ) وفي آية أخرى : ( قدرنا إنها لمن الغابرين ) أي حكمنا .
When you know this, we say: We do not concede that what is meant by 'qadar' is creation. Rather, the word 'qadar' may also be used for writing. The poet said: 'And know that the Majestic One has decreed in the first scriptures, which were written.' And it is also used for judgment. God said: 'Except his wife, we decreed (qaddarnaha) her to be among those who remain behind' (27:57) and in another verse: 'We decreed (qaddarna) that she is among those who remain behind' (15:60), meaning 'We judged.'
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection regarding the meaning of 'qadar' in the hadith. The author denies that it necessarily means 'creation' and provides linguistic evidence that 'qadar' can mean 'writing' or 'judgment.' A poet's line is cited showing that God has 'decreed' in the scriptures. Then two Quranic verses about Lot's wife are quoted, where 'qaddarna' is interpreted as 'We judged,' indicating a judicial decree. Thus, the word has a broader semantic range, and in the hadith it should be understood accordingly, not narrowly as creation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'qadar' only refers to God's creative power.
Correction: The passage shows 'qadar' also refers to writing (in scriptures) and judgment (divine decree).

Checkpoint
What alternative meaning of 'qadar' is supported by the verse about Lot's wife?
A) Creation
B) Writing
C) Judgment
D) Guidance

Key Takeaway
Qadar in Islamic theology is not limited to creation; it includes divine writing and judicial decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'it was qadar', do you only think of creation, or do you also acknowledge God's judgment and decree? How does this expand your understanding of God's sovereignty?
?
In what areas of your life do you need to submit to God's judgment (not just His creation)? What specific decision are you avoiding because you forget that Allah judges with wisdom?
?
If qadar includes writing and judgment, what does that imply about the certainty of God's decree? How should this affect your anxiety about the future?
